# IMPORTANT: Source Code Access

**Local source files are not present.** Your workspace does not contain source code. You **MUST** use Sourcegraph MCP tools to discover, read, and understand code before making any changes.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Read full file | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# Envoy Upstream Connection Pool Overflow

## Your Task

Envoy logs show 'upstream connection pool overflow' errors during traffic spikes. Find the C++ source files in envoyproxy/envoy that (1) define the overflow state in the connection pool and (2) emit or handle this overflow condition.

## Context

You are working on a codebase task involving repos from the incident domain.

## Available Resources

The local `/workspace/` directory contains: sg-evals/envoy--v1.31.2, sg-evals/data-plane-api--84e84367, sg-evals/go-control-plane--71637ad6, sg-evals/grpc--957dba5e.

**Note:** Additional repositories are accessible via Sourcegraph MCP tools:
- `sg-evals/envoy--v1.31.2` (envoyproxy/envoy)

I've completed the analysis of Envoy's upstream connection pool overflow handling. Here's a summary of my findings:

## Key Files Found

I identified **10 critical C++ source files** that define and handle the upstream connection pool overflow condition:

**Core Files:**
1. **`envoy/common/conn_pool.h`** - Defines the `PoolFailureReason::Overflow` enum
2. **`source/common/conn_pool/conn_pool_base.h`** - Base class for all connection pools
3. **`source/common/conn_pool/conn_pool_base.cc`** - Core implementation with overflow detection logic
4. **`source/common/upstream/resource_manager_impl.h`** - Resource limit enforcement

**Protocol-Specific Implementations:**
5. **`source/common/http/conn_pool_base.h`** - HTTP base class
6. **`source/common/http/http1/conn_pool.h`** - HTTP/1 specific
7. **`source/common/http/http2/conn_pool.h`** - HTTP/2 specific
8. **`source/common/http/http3/conn_pool.h`** - HTTP/3 specific
9. **`source/common/tcp/conn_pool.h`** - TCP specific

**Metrics & Logging:**
10. **`source/common/stream_info/utility.h`** - Response flag handling

## Overflow Handling Locations

The overflow condition is detected and handled in three key methods in `source/common/conn_pool/conn_pool_base.cc`:

1. **`tryCreateNewConnection()`** (line 138) - Increments `upstream_cx_overflow_` counter
2. **`attachStreamToClient()`** (lines 175-180) - Handles max streams overflow
3. **`newStreamImpl()`** (lines 282-287) - Handles max pending requests overflow

All three call `onPoolFailure()` with `PoolFailureReason::Overflow` when limits are exceeded.

CloseExcess is largely useful for callers that never\\n19:   // re-use connections (e.g. by closing rather than releasing connections). Using CloseExcess in\\n20:   // this situation guarantees that no idle connections will be held open by the conn pool awaiting\\n21:   // a connection stream.\\n22:   CloseExcess,\\n23: };\\n24: \\n25: /**\\n26:  * Handle that allows a pending connection or stream to be canceled before it is completed.\\n27:  */\\n28: class Cancellable {\\n29: public:\\n30:   virtual ~Cancellable() = default;\\n31: \\n32:   /**\\n33:    * Cancel the pending connection or stream.\\n34:    * @param cancel_policy a CancelPolicy that controls the behavior of this cancellation.\\n35:    */\\n36:   virtual void cancel(CancelPolicy cancel_policy) PURE;\\n37: };\\n38: \\n39: /**\\n40:  * Controls the behavior when draining a connection pool.\\n41:  */\\n42: enum class DrainBehavior {\\n43:   // Starts draining a pool, by gracefully completing all requests and gracefully closing all\\n44:   // connections, in preparation for deletion. It is invalid to create new streams or\\n45:   // connections from this pool after draining a pool with this behavior.\\n46:   DrainAndDelete,\\n47:   // Actively drain all existing connection pool connections. anticipate_incoming_stream) {\\n56:   // This is set to true any time global preconnect is being calculated.\\n57:   // ClusterManagerImpl::maybePreconnect is called directly before a stream is created, so the\\n58:   // stream must be anticipated.\\n59:   //\\n60:   // Also without this, we would never pre-establish a connection as the first\\n61:   // connection in a pool because pending/active streams could both be 0.\\n62:   int anticipated_streams = anticipate_incoming_stream ? 1 : 0;\\n63: \\n64:   // The number of streams we want to be provisioned for is the number of\\n65:   // pending, active, and anticipated streams times the preconnect ratio.\\n66:   // The number of streams we are (theoretically) provisioned for is the\\n67:   // connecting stream capacity plus the number of active streams.\\n68:   //\\n69:   // If preconnect ratio is not set, it defaults to 1, and this simplifies to the\\n70:   // legacy value of pending_streams_.size() > connecting_stream_capacity_\\n71:   return (pending_streams + active_streams + anticipated_streams) * preconnect_ratio >\\n72:          connecting_and_connected_capacity + active_streams;\\n73: }\\n74: \\n75: bool ConnPoolImplBase::shouldCreateNewConnection(float global_preconnect_ratio) const {\\n76:   // If the host is not healthy, don't make it do extra work, especially as\\n77:   // upstream selection logic may result in bypassing this upstream entirely.\\n78:   // If an Envoy user wants preconnecting for degraded upstreams this could be\\n79:   // added later via extending the global\\n87:     // preconnect limit, preconnect.\\n88:     // For global preconnect, we anticipate an incoming stream to this pool, since it is\\n89:     // prefetching for the next upcoming stream, which will likely be assigned to this pool.\\n90:     // We may eventually want to track preconnect_attempts to allow more preconnecting for\\n91:     // heavily weighted upstreams or sticky
